     <p>
      <b>
       National Public Radio
      </b>
      (usually shortened to
      <b>
       NPR
      </b>
      , stylized as
      <b>
       npr
      </b>
      ) is an American privately and publicly funded non-profit membership media organization that serves as a national
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_syndication" title="Radio syndication">
       syndicator
      </a>
      to a network of 900
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_radio" title="Public radio">
       public radio
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_NPR_stations" title="List of NPR stations">
       stations
      </a>
      in the United States of America.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      NPR produces and distributes news and cultural programming. Individual public radio stations are not required to broadcast all NPR programs that are produced. Most public radio stations broadcast a mixture of NPR programs, content from rival providers
      <a href="/wiki/American_Public_Media" title="American Public Media">
       American Public Media
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_International" title="Public Radio International">
       Public Radio International
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_Exchange" title="Public Radio Exchange">
       Public Radio Exchange
      </a>
      , and locally produced programs. NPR's flagships are two
      <a href="/wiki/Drive_time" title="Drive time">
       drive time
      </a>
      news broadcasts,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Morning_Edition" title="Morning Edition">
        Morning Edition
       </a>
      </i>
      and the afternoon
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/All_Things_Considered" title="All Things Considered">
        All Things Considered
       </a>
      </i>
      ; both are carried by most NPR member stations, and are two of the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_most-listened-to_radio_programs" title="List of most-listened-to radio programs">
       most popular radio programs
      </a>
      in the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      NPR manages the
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_Satellite_System" title="Public Radio Satellite System">
       Public Radio Satellite System
      </a>
      , which distributes NPR programs and other programming from independent producers and networks such as American Public Media and Public Radio International. Its content is also available on-demand via the web, mobile, and podcasts.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
       Name
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The organization's legal name is
      <i>
       National Public Radio
      </i>
      and its
      <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">
       trademarked
      </a>
      brand is
      <i>
       NPR
      </i>
      ; it is known by both names.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Name_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Name-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2010, the organization announced that it was "making a conscious effort to consistently refer to ourselves as NPR on-air and online" because NPR is the common name for the organization and the tag line "This ... is NPR" has been used by its radio hosts for many years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Name_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Name-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However,
      <i>
       National Public Radio
      </i>
      remains the legal name of the group, as it has been for more than 45 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Name_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Name-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">
       1970s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1970s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NPR_1970s_logo.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="136" data-file-width="226" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/NPR_1970s_logo.jpg/130px-NPR_1970s_logo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/NPR_1970s_logo.jpg/195px-NPR_1970s_logo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e7/NPR_1970s_logo.jpg 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NPR_1970s_logo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        1970s logo
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      National Public Radio replaced the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Educational_Radio_Network" title="National Educational Radio Network">
       National Educational Radio Network
      </a>
      on February 26, 1970, following congressional passage of the
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Act_of_1967" title="Public Broadcasting Act of 1967">
       Public Broadcasting Act of 1967
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JARVIK_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JARVIK-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This act was signed into law by
      <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">
       President
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">
       Lyndon B. Johnson
      </a>
      , and established the
      <a href="/wiki/Corporation_for_Public_Broadcasting" title="Corporation for Public Broadcasting">
       Corporation for Public Broadcasting
      </a>
      , which also created the
      <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">
       Public Broadcasting Service
      </a>
      in addition to NPR. A CPB organizing committee under John Witherspoon first created a Board of Directors chaired by
      <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Mayes" title="Bernard Mayes">
       Bernard Mayes
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The board then hired
      <a href="/wiki/Don_Quayle" title="Don Quayle">
       Donald Quayle
      </a>
      to be the first president of NPR with 30 employees and 90 charter member stations, and studios in Washington, D.C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      NPR aired its first broadcast in April 1971, covering
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">
       United States Senate
      </a>
      hearings on the
      <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">
       Vietnam War
      </a>
      . A month later, the afternoon drive-time newscast
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/All_Things_Considered" title="All Things Considered">
        All Things Considered
       </a>
      </i>
      began, on May 3, 1971, first hosted by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Conley_(reporter)" title="Robert Conley (reporter)">
       Robert Conley
      </a>
      . NPR was primarily a production and distribution organization until 1977, when it merged with the
      <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Public_Radio_Stations" title="Association of Public Radio Stations">
       Association of Public Radio Stations
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">
       1980s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1980s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      NPR suffered an almost fatal setback in 1983 when efforts to expand services created a deficit of nearly US$7 million. After a Congressional investigation and the resignation of NPR's president,
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Mankiewicz" title="Frank Mankiewicz">
       Frank Mankiewicz
      </a>
      , the Corporation for Public Broadcasting agreed to lend the network money in order to stave off bankruptcy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In exchange, NPR agreed to a new arrangement whereby the annual CPB stipend that it had previously received directly would be divided among local stations instead; in turn, those stations would support NPR productions on a subscription basis. NPR also agreed to turn its satellite service into a cooperative venture (the
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_Satellite_System" title="Public Radio Satellite System">
       Public Radio Satellite System
      </a>
      ), making it possible for non-NPR shows to get national distribution. It took NPR approximately three years to pay off the debt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NPRLogoOld.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="77" data-file-width="194" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/26/NPRLogoOld.png/150px-NPRLogoOld.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/26/NPRLogoOld.png 1.5x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NPRLogoOld.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        1990s logo
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">
       1990s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Delano_Lewis" title="Delano Lewis">
       Delano Lewis
      </a>
      , the president of
      <a href="/wiki/C%26P_Telephone" title="C&amp;P Telephone">
       C&amp;P Telephone
      </a>
      , left that position to become NPR's CEO and president in January 1994.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1994_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lewis1994-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lewis resigned in August 1998.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis1994_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lewis1994-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klose1998_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-klose1998-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In November 1998, NPR's board of directors hired
      <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Klose" title="Kevin Klose">
       Kevin Klose
      </a>
      , the director of the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Broadcasting_Bureau" title="International Broadcasting Bureau">
       International Broadcasting Bureau
      </a>
      , as its president and chief executive officer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klose1998_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-klose1998-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">
       2000s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2000s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
      <tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
       <td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">
        “
       </td>
       <td style="padding:0 10px;">
        <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
         September 11th
        </a>
        made it apparent in a very urgent way that we need another facility that could keep NPR going if something devastating happens in Washington.
       </td>
       <td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">
        ”
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;">
        <cite>
         — Jay Kernis, NPR's senior VP for programming
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nprwest2002_11-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-nprwest2002-11">
           [11]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      NPR spent nearly $13 million to acquire and equip a
      <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">
       West Coast
      </a>
      25,000-square-foot (2,300 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) production facility,
      <b>
       NPR West
      </b>
      , which opened in
      <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">
       Culver City
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California" title="Los Angeles County, California">
       Los Angeles County, California
      </a>
      , in November 2002. With room for up to 90 employees, it was established to expand its production capabilities, improve its coverage of the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">
       western United States
      </a>
      , and create a backup production facility capable of keeping NPR on the air in the event of a catastrophe in Washington, D.C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nprwest2002_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nprwest2002-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In November 2003, NPR received US$235 million from the estate of the late
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joan_B._Kroc" title="Joan B. Kroc">
       Joan B. Kroc
      </a>
      , the widow of
      <a href="/wiki/Ray_Kroc" title="Ray Kroc">
       Ray Kroc
      </a>
      , founder of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_Corporation" title="McDonald's Corporation">
       McDonald's Corporation
      </a>
      . This was the largest monetary gift ever to a cultural institution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2004 NPR's budget increased by over 50% to US$153 million due to the Kroc gift. US$34 million of the money was deposited in its
      <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">
       endowment
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The endowment fund before the gift totaled $35 million. NPR will use the interest from the bequest to expand its news staff and reduce some member stations' fees.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nyt-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 2005 budget was about US$120 million.
     </p>
     <p>
      In August 2005, NPR entered podcasting with a directory of over 170 programs created by NPR and member stations. By November of that year, users downloaded NPR and other public radio podcasts 5 million times. Ten years later, by March 2015, users downloaded podcasts produced only by NPR 94 million times,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and NPR podcasts like Fresh Air and TED Radio Hour routinely made the
      <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">
       iTunes
      </a>
      Top Podcasts list.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ken_Stern" title="Ken Stern">
       Ken Stern
      </a>
      became chief executive in September 2006, reportedly as the "hand-picked successor" of CEO Kevin Klose, who gave up the job but remained as NPR's president; Stern had worked with Klose at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe" title="Radio Free Europe">
       Radio Free Europe
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpost2008_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-washpost2008-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On December 10, 2008, NPR announced that it would reduce its workforce by 7% and cancel the news programs
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Day_to_Day" title="Day to Day">
        Day to Day
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/News_%26_Notes" title="News &amp; Notes">
        News &amp; Notes
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPRTCSPla_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPRTCSPla-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The organization indicated this was in response to a rapid drop in corporate
      <a href="/wiki/Underwriting_spot" title="Underwriting spot">
       underwriting
      </a>
      in the wake of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_crisis_of_2008" title="Economic crisis of 2008">
       economic crisis of 2008
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPRTCSPla_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPRTCSPla-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the fall of 2008, NPR programming reached a record 27.5 million people weekly, according to Arbitron ratings figures. NPR stations reach 32.7 million listeners overall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-audiencefigures_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-audiencefigures-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In March 2008, the NPR Board announced that Stern would be stepping down from his role as Chief Executive Officer, following conflict with NPR's Board of Directors "over the direction of the organization" (including issues NPR's member station managers had had with NPR's expansion into
      <a href="/wiki/New_media" title="New media">
       new media
      </a>
      "at the expense of serving" the stations that financially support NPR).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpost2008_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-washpost2008-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 2009, corporate sponsorship made up 26% of the NPR budget.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-npr-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">
       2010s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2010s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2847" data-file-width="2077" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg/170px-National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg/255px-National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg/340px-National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Public_Radio_headquarters.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        NPR's former headquarters at 635
        <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">
         Massachusetts Avenue
        </a>
        NW in Washington, D.C. (demolished in 2013)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="1440" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG/170px-2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG/255px-2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG/340px-2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2015_Supreme_Court_Editathon_18.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The new NPR sign at 1111 North Capital St, NE.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In October 2010, NPR accepted a $1.8 million grant from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Society_Institute" title="Open Society Institute">
       Open Society Institute
      </a>
      . The grant is meant to begin a project called Impact of Government that was intended to add at least 100 journalists at NPR member radio stations in all 50 states by 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSoros_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYTSoros-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The OSI has made previous donations, but does not take on air credit for its gifts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiu_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chiu-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In April 2013, NPR moved from its home of 19 years (635
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">
       Massachusetts Avenue
      </a>
      NW) to new offices and production facilities at 1111 North Capitol Street NE in a building adapted from the former
      <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Potomac_Telephone_Company_Warehouse_and_Repair_Facility" title="Chesapeake and Potomac Telephone Company Warehouse and Repair Facility">
       C&amp;P Telephone Warehouse and Repair Facility
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The new headquarters—at the corner of North Capitol Street NE and L Street NW—is in the burgeoning
      <a href="/wiki/NoMa" title="NoMa">
       NoMa
      </a>
      neighborhood of Washington.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first show scheduled to be broadcast from the new studios was
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Weekend_Edition" title="Weekend Edition">
        Weekend Edition Saturday
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Morning_Edition" title="Morning Edition">
        Morning Edition
       </a>
      </i>
      was the last show to move to the new location.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2013 NPR canceled the weekday call-in show
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Talk_of_the_Nation" title="Talk of the Nation">
        Talk of the Nation
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In September 2013, certain of NPR's 840 full- and part-time employees were offered a
      <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_redundancy" title="Voluntary redundancy">
       voluntary buyout
      </a>
      plan, with the goal of reducing staff by 10 percent and returning NPR to a balanced budget by the 2015
      <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">
       fiscal year
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">
       Governance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Governance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      NPR is a membership organization. Member stations are required to be
      <a href="/wiki/Non-commercial" title="Non-commercial">
       non-commercial
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Non-commercial_educational" title="Non-commercial educational">
       non-commercial educational
      </a>
      radio stations; have at least five full-time professional employees; operate for at least 18 hours per day; and not be designed solely to further a
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_broadcasting" title="Religious broadcasting">
       religious broadcasting
      </a>
      philosophy or be used for classroom
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distance_learning" title="Distance learning">
       distance learning
      </a>
      programming. Each member station receives one vote at the annual NPR board meetings—exercised by its designated Authorized Station Representative ("A-Rep").
     </p>
     <p>
      To oversee the day-to-day operations and prepare its budget, members elect a Board of Directors. This board is composed of ten A-Reps, five members of the general public, and the chair of the NPR Foundation. Terms are for three years and are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staggered_board_of_directors" title="Staggered board of directors">
       staggered
      </a>
      such that some stand for election every year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of March 2015
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , the Board of Directors of NPR included the following members:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_Board_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPR_Board-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       NPR Member Station Managers
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Mike Crane, Director,
       <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Public_Radio" title="Wisconsin Public Radio">
        Wisconsin Public Radio
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Betsy Gardella, President and CEO,
       <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Public_Radio" title="New Hampshire Public Radio">
        New Hampshire Public Radio
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Kit Jensen, Chief Operating Officer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WVIZ/PBS" title="WVIZ/PBS">
        WVIZ/PBS
       </a>
       &amp; 90.3
       <a href="/wiki/WCPN" title="WCPN">
        WCPN
       </a>
       ideastream, Chair of the Board
      </li>
      <li>
       Roger LaMay, General Manager,
       <a href="/wiki/WXPN" title="WXPN">
        WXPN
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>

       <a href="/wiki/KUOW-FM" title="KUOW-FM">
        KUOW
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>

       <a href="/wiki/WSIU_(FM)" title="WSIU (FM)">
        WSIU Public Broadcasting
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Florence Rogers, President and General Manager,
       <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Public_Radio" title="Nevada Public Radio">
        Nevada Public Radio
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Mike Savage, General Manager,
       <a href="/wiki/WBAA" title="WBAA">
        WBAA
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Kerry Swanson, Station Manager,
       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Public_Radio" title="Northwest Public Radio">
        Northwest Public Radio
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       Connie Walker, General Manager,
       <a href="/wiki/WUNC_(FM)" title="WUNC (FM)">
        WUNC
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>

       President of NPR
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Jarl_Mohn" title="Jarl Mohn">
        Jarl Mohn
       </a>
       , President and CEO
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Chair of the NPR Foundation
      </dt>
     </dl>

      <li>

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starbucks_Coffee_Company" title="Starbucks Coffee Company">
        Starbucks Coffee Company
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Public Members of the Board
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Fabiola Arredondo, Managing Partner,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Siempre_Holdings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Siempre Holdings (page does not exist)">
        Siempre Holdings
       </a>
      </li>

      </li>
      <li>

       <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">

       </a>

       Paul G. Haaga, Jr., Chairman of the Board, Retired,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Capital_Research_and_Management_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Capital Research and Management Company (page does not exist)">
        Capital Research and Management Company
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       John S. Wotowicz, Managing Partner of
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Concentric_Capital&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Concentric Capital (page does not exist)">
        Concentric Capital
       </a>
       , Vice Chair of the Board
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The original purposes of NPR, as ratified by the Board of Directors, are the following:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Provide an identifiable daily product which is consistent and reflects the highest standards of broadcast journalism.
      </li>
      <li>
       Provide extended coverage of public events, issues and ideas, and to acquire and produce special public affairs programs.
      </li>
      <li>
       Acquire and produce cultural programs which can be scheduled individually by stations.
      </li>
      <li>
       Provide access to the intellectual and cultural resources of cities, universities and rural districts through a system of cooperative program development with member public radio stations.
      </li>
      <li>
       Develop and distribute programs for specific groups (
       <a href="/wiki/Adult_education" title="Adult education">
        adult education
       </a>
       , instruction, modular units for local productions) which may meet needs of individual regions or groups, but may not have general national relevance.
      </li>
      <li>
       Establish liaison with foreign broadcasters for a program exchange service.
      </li>
      <li>
       Produce materials specifically intended to develop the art and technical potential of radio
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       NPR Ombudsman/Public Editor
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.npr.org/people/384352444/elizabeth-jensen" rel="nofollow">
        Elizabeth Jensen
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The Ombudsman/Public Editor responds to significant listener queries, comments and criticisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elizabeth_Jensen_home_page_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Elizabeth_Jensen_home_page-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The position reports to the President and CEO
      <a href="/wiki/Jarl_Mohn" title="Jarl Mohn">
       Jarl Mohn
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Elizabeth Jensen was appointed to a three-year term in January 2015.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elizabeth_Jensen_home_page_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Elizabeth_Jensen_home_page-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">
       Funding
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Funding">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 2010, NPR revenues totaled $180 million, with the bulk of revenues coming from programming fees,
      <a href="/wiki/Grant_(money)" title="Grant (money)">
       grants
      </a>
      from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundation_(charity)" title="Foundation (charity)">
       foundations
      </a>
      or business entities, contributions and
      <a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">
       sponsorships
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-npr-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the 2009 financial statement, about 50% of NPR revenues come from the fees it charges member stations for programming and distribution charges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_20-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-npr-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Typically, NPR member stations receive funds through on-air
      <a href="/wiki/Pledge_drive" title="Pledge drive">
       pledge drives
      </a>
      , corporate underwriting, state and local governments, educational institutions, and the federally funded Corporation for Public Broadcasting (CPB). In 2009, member stations derived 6% of their revenue from federal, state and local government funding, 10% of their revenue from
      <a href="/wiki/Corporation_for_Public_Broadcasting" title="Corporation for Public Broadcasting">
       CPB
      </a>
      grants, and 14% of their revenue from universities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_20-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-npr-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_Responds_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPR_Responds-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While NPR does not receive any direct federal funding, it does receive a small number of competitive grants from CPB and federal agencies like the Department of Education and the Department of Commerce. This funding amounts to approximately 2% of NPR's overall revenues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_20-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-npr-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 1970s and early 1980s, the majority of NPR funding came from the federal government. Steps were taken during the 1980s to completely wean NPR from government support, but
      <a href="#History">
       the 1983 funding crisis
      </a>
      forced the network to make immediate changes. According to
      <a href="/wiki/Corporation_for_Public_Broadcasting" title="Corporation for Public Broadcasting">
       CPB
      </a>
      , in 2009 11.3% of the aggregate revenues of all public radio broadcasting stations were funded from federal sources, principally through CPB;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPB2009_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CPB2009-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in 2012 10.9% of the revenues for Public Radio came from federal sources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPB2012_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CPB2012-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2011, NPR announced the roll-out of their own online
      <a href="/wiki/Advertising_network" title="Advertising network">
       advertising network
      </a>
      , which allows member stations to run geographically
      <a href="/wiki/Targeted_advertising" title="Targeted advertising">
       targeted advertisement
      </a>
      spots from national sponsors that may otherwise be unavailable to their local area, opening additional revenue streams to the broadcaster.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      2013 saw the launch of
      <i>
       Center Stage
      </i>
      , a mix of
      <a href="/wiki/Native_advertising" title="Native advertising">
       native advertising
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banner_ad" title="Banner ad">
       banner ad
      </a>
      featured prominently on the NPR homepage, above-the-fold. The launch partner for
      <i>
       Center Stage
      </i>
      was
      <a href="/wiki/Squarespace" title="Squarespace">
       Squarespace
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2014, NPR CEO Jarl Mohn said the network would begin to increase revenue by having brands NPR views as more relevant to the audience underwrite NPR programs and requesting higher rates from them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Underwriting_spots_vs._commercials">
       Underwriting spots vs. commercials
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Underwriting spots vs. commercials">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In contrast with
      <a href="/wiki/Commercial_broadcasting" title="Commercial broadcasting">
       commercial broadcasting
      </a>
      , NPR's radio broadcasts do not carry traditional commercials, but has advertising in the form of brief statements from major sponsors which may include corporate
      <a href="/wiki/Advertising_slogan" title="Advertising slogan">
       slogans
      </a>
      , descriptions of products and services, contact information such as website addresses and
      <a href="/wiki/Telephone_number" title="Telephone number">
       telephone numbers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These statements are called
      <a href="/wiki/Underwriting_spot" title="Underwriting spot">
       underwriting spots
      </a>
      and, unlike commercials, are governed by specific FCC restrictions in addition to
      <a href="/wiki/False_advertising" title="False advertising">
       truth in advertising
      </a>
      laws; they cannot advocate a product or "promote the goods and services" of for-profit entities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These restrictions apply only to radio broadcasts and not NPR's other digital platforms. When questioned on the subject of how corporate underwriting revenues and foundation grants were holding up during the recession, in a speech broadcast on
      <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">
       C-SPAN
      </a>
      before the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(United_States)" title="National Press Club (United States)">
       National Press Club
      </a>
      on March 2, 2009, then President and CEO
      <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Schiller" title="Vivian Schiller">
       Vivian Schiller
      </a>
      stated: "underwriting is down, it's down for everybody; this is the area that is most down for us, in sponsorship, underwriting, advertising, call it whatever you want; just like it is for all of media."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hosts of the NPR program
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Planet_Money" title="Planet Money">
        Planet Money
       </a>
      </i>
      stated the audience is indeed a product being sold to advertisers in the same way as commercial stations, saying: "they are not advertisers exactly but, they have a lot of the same characteristics; let's just say that."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Audience">
       Audience
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Audience">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A Harris telephone survey conducted in 2005 found that NPR was the most trusted news source in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to 2009, NPR statistics, about 20.9 million listeners tune into NPR each week.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to 2015 figures, 87% of the NPR terrestrial public radio audience and 67% of the NPR podcast audience is white.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the 2012
      <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">
       Pew Research Center
      </a>
      2012 News Consumption Survey, NPR listeners tend to be highly educated, with 54% of regular listeners being college graduates and 21% having some college.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pew-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      NPR's audience is almost exactly average in terms of the sex of listeners (49% male, 51% female).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pew-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      NPR listeners have higher incomes than average (the 2012 Pew study showed that 43% earn over $75,000, 27% earn between $30,000 and $75,000).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pew-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Pew survey found that the NPR audience tends Democratic (17% Republican, 37% independent, 43% Democratic) and liberal (21% conservative, 39% moderate, 36% liberal).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_47-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pew-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      NPR stations generally do not subscribe to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbitron" title="Arbitron">
       Arbitron
      </a>
      rating service and are not included in published ratings and rankings such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_%26_Records" title="Radio &amp; Records">
        Radio &amp; Records
       </a>
      </i>
      . However, NPR station listenership is measured by Arbitron in both Diary and PPM (people meter) markets. NPR stations are frequently not included in "summary level" diary data used by most advertising agencies for media planning. Data on NPR listening can be accessed using "respondent level" diary data. Additionally, all radio stations (public and commercial) are treated equally within the PPM data sets making NPR station listenership data much more widely available to the media planning community. According to Arbitron's National Broadcast Audience Estimate report for September 29, 2011,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      NPR's signature morning news program,
      <i>
       Morning Edition
      </i>
      , is the network's most popular program, drawing 12.9 million listeners a week, with its afternoon newsmagazine,
      <i>
       All Things Considered
      </i>
      , a close second, with 12.2 million listeners a week. Arbitron data is also provided by
      <a href="/wiki/Radio_Research_Consortium" title="Radio Research Consortium">
       Radio Research Consortium
      </a>
      , a non-profit corporation which subscribes to the Arbitron service and distributes the data to NPR and other non-commercial stations and on its website.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfcron_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sfcron-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_media">
       Digital media
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Digital media">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      NPR's history in
      <a href="/wiki/Digital_media" title="Digital media">
       digital media
      </a>
      includes the work of an independent, for-profit company called Public Interactive, which was founded in 1999
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRINPR2008_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PRINPR2008-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and acquired by
      <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_International" title="Public Radio International">
       PRI
      </a>
      in June 2004, when it became a non-profit company.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nprDS2004_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nprDS2004-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By July 2008, Public Interactive had "170 subscribers who collectively operate 325 public radio and television stations" and clients such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Car_Talk" title="Car Talk">
        Car Talk
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_World_(radio_program)" title="The World (radio program)">
        The World
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Tavis_Smiley_Show" title="The Tavis Smiley Show">
        The Tavis Smiley Show
       </a>
      </i>
      ; by the end of that month, NPR acquired Public Interactive from PRI
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRINPR2008_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PRINPR2008-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In March 2011, NPR revealed a restructuring proposal in which Boston-based Public Interactive would become NPR Digital Services, separate from the Washington D.C.-based NPR Digital Media, which focuses on NPR-branded services.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPRDigital2011_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPRDigital2011-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      NPR Digital Services would continue offering its services to public TV stations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPRDigital2011_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPRDigital2011-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The technical backbone of its digital news publishing system is
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Core_Publisher&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Core Publisher (page does not exist)">
       Core Publisher
      </a>
      , which was built on
      <a href="/wiki/Drupal" title="Drupal">
       Drupal
      </a>
      , an open-source
      <a href="/wiki/Content_management_system" title="Content management system">
       content management system
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPRDigital2011_52-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-NPRDigital2011-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        Kinsey Wilson and the npr.org crew at the 69th Annual Peabody Awards
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      NPR has been dubbed as "leveraging the Twitter generation",
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE">
         <span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (June 2016)">
          this quote needs a citation
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      because of its adaptation of the popular
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microblog" title="Microblog">
       microblogging
      </a>
      service as one of its primary vehicles of information. Of NPR's Twitter followers, the majority (67%) still do listen to NPR on the radio. According to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mashable.com" title="Mashable.com">
       Mashable.com
      </a>
      , in a survey of more than 10,000 respondents, NPR found that its
      <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">
       Twitter
      </a>
      followers are younger, more connected to the
      <a href="/wiki/Social_web" title="Social web">
       social web
      </a>
      , and more likely to access content through digital platforms such as its
      <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">
       Peabody Award
      </a>
      -winning website npr.org, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Podcast" title="Podcast">
       podcasts
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">
       mobile apps
      </a>
      and more.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      NPR has more than one Twitter account including @NPR;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      its survey found that most respondents followed between two and five NPR accounts, including topical account, show-specific accounts and on-air staff accounts.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In addition, NPR's
      <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">
       Facebook
      </a>
      page has been at the forefront of the company foray into social media. Started by college student and fan Geoff Campbell
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in 2008, the page was quickly taken over by the organization,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and over the last two years has grown to nearly 4 million fans and is a popular example of the company's new focus on a younger audience.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       Programs produced by NPR
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="News_and_public_affairs_programs">
       News and public affairs programs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=NPR&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: News and public affairs programs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:136px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NPR_News_logo.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="91" data-file-width="134" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/NPR_News_logo.png" width="134"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NPR_News_logo.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        NPR News logo
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      NPR produces a morning and an afternoon news program, both of which also have weekend editions with different hosts. It also produces hourly news briefs around the clock. NPR formerly distributed the
      <a href="/wiki/WRN_Broadcast" title="WRN Broadcast">
       World Radio Network
      </a>
      , a daily compilation of news reports from international radio news, but no longer does so.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>

        </a>

        Robert Siegel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Audie_Cornish" title="Audie Cornish">
        Audie Cornish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kelly_McEvers" title="Kelly McEvers">
        Kelly McEvers
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ari_Shapiro" title="Ari Shapiro">

       </a>

        </a>
       </sup>
